# APPENDIX 8.2

# Perturbation Technique

The perturbation technique developed below is a very simplified approach in obtaining reactivity changes incurred through small perturbations in cross sections, away from the critical parameters.

Upon making diffusion theory approximation to the current $J$ and assuming a solution for a bare one region three group system of the form $(\nabla^2 + B^2)\phi = 0$ , one obtains the following steady state equations.

$$
\begin{array}{l} (D _ {1} B _ {1} ^ {2} + \Sigma_ {a 1} + \Sigma_ {r 1}) \phi_ {1} \quad - \nabla) (\Sigma_ {f 1} \phi_ {1} + \Sigma_ {f 2} \phi_ {2} + \Sigma_ {f 3} \phi_ {3}) = 0 \\ \left(D _ {2} B _ {2} ^ {2} + \sum_ {a 2} + \sum_ {r 2}\right) \phi_ {2} - \sum_ {r 1} \phi_ {1} = 0 \\ \left(\mathrm {D} _ {3} \mathrm {B} _ {3} ^ {2} + \sum_ {\mathrm {a} 3}\right) \phi_ {1} - \sum_ {\mathrm {r} 2} \phi_ {2} = 0 \\ \end{array}
$$

The steady state solution is

$$
1 = \left\{ \begin{array}{l} \frac {\Sigma_ {f 3}}{\Sigma_ {a 3}} \left(\frac {\Sigma_ {r 2}}{\Sigma_ {r 2} + \Sigma_ {a 2}}\right) \left(\frac {\Sigma_ {r 1}}{\Sigma_ {r 1} + \Sigma_ {a 1}}\right) \cdot \frac {1}{\left(B _ {3} ^ {2} L _ {3} ^ {2} + 1\right)} + \frac {\Sigma_ {f 2}}{\left(\Sigma_ {a 2} + \Sigma_ {r 2}\right)} \cdot \frac {\Sigma_ {r 1}}{\left(\Sigma_ {a 1} + \Sigma_ {r 1}\right)} \\ \hline B _ {1} ^ {2} \tau_ {1} + 1 - \frac {\gamma \Sigma_ {f} ^ {1}}{\Sigma_ {a 1} + \Sigma_ {r 1}} \Bigg \} \\ \left\{B _ {2} ^ {2} \tau_ {2} + 1 \right\} \end{array} \right\}
$$

$$
\tau_ {i} = \frac {D _ {i}}{\sum_ {a i} + \sum_ {r i}} \quad L ^ {2} _ {i} = \left\{\frac {D _ {i}}{\sum_ {a i}} \right\}
$$

Define:

$$
\eta_ {1} = \frac {\sqrt {\sum_ {f l}}}{(\sum_ {a l} + \sum_ {r l})}
$$

$$
\eta_ {2} = \frac {\gamma \Sigma_ {f 2}}{\left(\Sigma_ {a 2} + \Sigma_ {r 2}\right)} \quad \frac {\Sigma_ {r l}}{\left(\Sigma_ {a l} + \Sigma_ {r l}\right)}
$$

$$
\eta_ {3} = \frac {\nu \Sigma_ {f 3}}{\Sigma_ {a 3}} \quad \left(\frac {\Sigma_ {r 2}}{\Sigma_ {a 2} + \Sigma_ {r 2}}\right) \quad \frac {\Sigma_ {r l}}{\left(\Sigma_ {a l} + \Sigma_ {r l}\right)}
$$

Now approximate the steady state solution as

$$
k \approx \frac {\left\{\frac {\eta_ {3}}{\left(B _ {3} ^ {2} L _ {3} ^ {2} + 1\right)} + \eta_ {2} \right\}}{\left\{B _ {1} ^ {2} \tau_ {1} + 1 - \eta_ {1} \right\} \left\{B _ {2} ^ {2} \tau_ {2} + 1 \right\}}
$$

Assume the system is perturbed by an amount $\delta \eta_{2}$ and $\delta \eta_{3}$ and neglect its effect upon $\tau$ and $L$ , resulting in a multiplication constant $k + \delta k$ .

Then:

$$
\frac {k + \delta k}{k} = 1 + \rho
$$

and

$$
\rho = \frac {\varepsilon \eta_ {3} + \varepsilon \eta_ {2} \left(\mathrm {B} _ {3} ^ {2} \mathrm {L} _ {3} ^ {2} + 1\right)}{\eta_ {3} + \eta_ {2} \left(\mathrm {B} _ {3} ^ {2} \mathrm {L} _ {3} ^ {2} + 1\right)}
$$

# APPENDIX 8.3

Burnup and Fission Product Poisons

Burnup:

Upon the burnup of one gram of U-235, 2.563 x $10^{21}$ atoms are destroyed. Of this number, $\left\langle \sigma_{\mathrm{f}} / \sigma_{\mathrm{a}} \right\rangle$ is the fraction destroyed by fission.

$$
\left\langle \frac {\sigma_ {f}}{\sigma_ {a}} \right\rangle = \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {i = 3} \psi_ {i} \left\langle \frac {\sigma_ {f}}{\sigma_ {a}} \right\rangle \frac {o}{o} \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {i = 3} \psi_ {i}
$$

where $\psi_{\mathbf{i}} =$ fraction of fissions in energy Group i.

$\left\langle \frac{\sigma_{\mathrm{f}}}{\sigma_{\mathrm{a}}} \right\rangle_{\mathrm{i}} = \frac{\text{Ratio of fission to absorption cross section for U-235 averaged over energy group i.}}{}$

<table><tr><td>i</td><td>&lt;σf/σa&gt; i</td><td>ψi</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>0.8988</td><td>.083</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>0.6495</td><td>.639</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>0.8445</td><td>.278</td></tr></table>

$$
\left\langle \frac {\sigma_ {f}}{\sigma_ {a}} \right\rangle_ {\text {s p e c t r u m}} = 0. 7 2 4 3
$$

Therefore one gram burnup of U-235 requires on the average 1.856 x $10^{21}$ fissions, and one full power hour of reactor operation at 125 MW is

equivalent to 7.515 grams U-235 destroyed. Inventory of U-235 within the reactor at any time T is expressed as

$$
M (T) = M _ {(0)} - 0. 0 0 7 5 1 5 T
$$

M is kgms T in full power hours. Also the concentration of U-235 per cm³ of fuel can be expressed relative to initial concentration as

$$
\rho (T) = \rho (0) \left\{1 - \frac {0 . 0 0 7 5 1 5 T}{M (o)} \right\}
$$

All cross section involving the fuel are written as functions of $\varrho$ , for example:

$$
\Sigma_ {a 3} = \left\{0. 0 0 1 6 5 + 0. 4 5 5 3 \rho \right\}
$$

# Fission Product Poisons

The additional absorption resulting from non-volatile fission products are approximated by the following assumptions.

1 fission = 100 barns equivalence of thermal poisons   
1 fission = 10 barns equivalence of intermediate poisons.

Then the added macroscopic absorption cross section for the core region are given as:

Core

$$
\Delta \Sigma_ {a 3} (T) = . 5 6 6 x 1 0 ^ {- 6} T \quad c m ^ {- 1}
$$

$$
\Delta \Sigma_ {a 2} ^ {\text {C o r e}} (T) = . 5 6 6 x 1 0 ^ {- 7} T \quad \mathrm {c m} ^ {- 1}
$$

T in full power hours.

The worth in terms of reactivity are calculated as function of $\mathbf{T}$ by the perturbation method described in Appendix 8.2.

# APPENDIX 8.4

# Prompt Neutron Lifetime

The following analysis is a relatively simple method of estimating prompt neutron lifetimes from multigroup constants for an unreflected system. Method in part is similar to that presented in Ref. 70.

Define:

Ti = average time a neutron spends in the $i^{\text{th}}$ energy group.

$\eta_{i} = \text{Relative number of neutrons existing in the } i^{th} \text{ group}$

Ni = Fraction of neutrons born in the $i^{\text{th}}$ group

$\overline{V} i = \text{Average neutron speed of the } i^{\text{th}}$ group.

Then:

$$
\mathrm {T i} = \frac {1}{\bar {v} _ {\mathrm {i}} \sum_ {\mathrm {t i}}}
$$

$$
\text {W h e r e} \quad \sum_ {\mathrm {t i}} = \sum_ {\mathrm {a i}} + \sum_ {\mathrm {x i}} + \mathrm {D} _ {\mathrm {i}} \mathrm {B} _ {\mathrm {i}} ^ {2}
$$

$$
\eta_ {i} = N i + \sum_ {j = 1} ^ {\left\{j = 1 - 1 \right\}} \left\{\frac {j}{p = 1} \quad \frac {\left(\sum_ {x i}\right)}{\sum_ {t i}} \right\} \quad N j
$$

Then the prompt neutron lifetime over all energy groups, $k$ in number is:

$$
\left\langle \mathrm {T} \right\rangle_ {\mathrm {k}} \cong \sum_ {\mathrm {i} 1} ^ {\mathrm {i} = \mathrm {k}} \frac {\mathrm {T i}}{\sum_ {\mathrm {i} = 1} ^ {\mathrm {i} = \mathrm {k}} \eta_ {\mathrm {i}}}
$$

# APPENDIX 11.1

# DESCRIPTION OF SIMULATION PROGRAM

The flow sheet of the system which was simulated is shown in Figure 11.2. Figures A-ll.l through A-ll.5 show the electrical circuits or roadmaps which were used to represent the fuel loop and heat transfer circuit l. The roadmaps for heat transfer circuit 2 are similar to but simpler than those for circuit l; they are now shown. The method for simulating reactor kinetics is well known and is not repeated here. See References 30 and 31.

Figures A-ll.1 shows the roadmap for the fuel loop. Amplifiers 1 and 2 represent passage of the fuel through the core. $\overline{\theta}_{\mathrm{fc}}$ is the mean fuel temperature in the core. Amplifiers 3 and 4 represent passage of the fuel through one primary heat exchanger. $\overline{\theta}_{\mathrm{fh}}$ is the mean fuel temperature in the heat exchanger. Amplifiers 5 and 6 simulate passage of fuel through the other primary heat exchanger.

Figure A-11.2 shows the roadmap for the salt side of the primary heat exchanger and the superheater. Amplifiers 9 and 10 represent passage of the salt through the primary heat exchanger. Terminals marked C, S. go to the control system circuits which are shown in Figure A-11.5. These will be described later. Amplifier 11 generates the salt temperature resulting when the by-passed salt mixes with the salt from the primary heat exchanger. The box labeled $\tau_{3}$ represents a time-lag device which simulates the transport delay in piping. Amplifiers 12 and 13 simulate salt passage through the superheater.

Amplifier 14 again generates a mixed-salt temperature (See Figure 11.2). Amplifiers 7 and 8 generate the coupling voltages between fuel and salt in the primary heat exchanger. The power transferred across the exchanger, $P_h$ is determined by the mean fuel and salt temperatures in the exchanger. The time constant of amplifier 7 represents, to some approximation, the heat capacity of the tube metal in the exchanger.

Figure A-11.3 shows the roadmap for the salt side of the steam generator. Amplifier 16 generates the mixed salt temperature going into the steam generator. Amplifiers 17 and 18 simulate passage of the salt through the steam generator.

Figure A-11.4 shows the method used for generating the power demand voltages. $P_s$ and $P_g$ are the power demands from the superheater and steam generator respectively. The ganged potentiometers may be set to any desired power demand. Amplifier 24 generates the output steam temperature. The assumption is made that steam temperature is proportional to the superheater inlet salt temperature and to the power extracted from the superheater. Here an effect rather than a physical phenomenon is being simulated.

Figure A-11.5 shows the manner in which the control system was simulated. A Brown recorder was used to display the output steam temperature $\theta_{\mathrm{v2}}$ . Limit switches were placed on this recorder in such a manner that when steam temperature varied from its design value by a certain threshold setting a voltage of proper polarity was applied to amplifier 25 through a gain setting potentiometer. Amplifier 25 integrated this error voltage to give $W_{s5}$ , the flow rate through the by-pass line. $W_{s5}$

is limited by the diodes in the feedback circuits around amplifier 25 to lie in the range of zero to 75 volts (zero to 1570 pounds/second flow rate). The voltage representing $W_{s5}$ drives a multiplier and the appropriate output connections are shown in Figures A-11.5 and A-11.2. $W_{s4}$ , the flow rate through the heat exchanger is generated by amplifier 26 as the difference between $W_{s1}$ (a constant represented by 100 volts) and $W_{s5}$ . $W_{s4}$ drives another multiplier as shown in Figure A-11.5.

# APPENDIX 11.2

# Expansion Chamber Heating Calculations

If fuel volume is taken to be 45 cubic feet, and fuel is pumped into reactor at $1100^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , as in the ARE, and raised to $1225^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ average operating temperature,

$$
\text {D e n s i t y} = 2 5 3. 0 - . 0 3 2 8 \mathrm {T} ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}) \mathrm {l b / f t} ^ {3}
$$

$$
\text {D e n s i t y} 1 1 0 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F} = 2 1 6. 9
$$

$$
\text {D e n s i t y} 1 2 2 5 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F} = 2 1 2. 8
$$

$$
\text {E x p a n s i o n} = 3 2 \mathrm {f t} ^ {3} \times 4. 1 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3} = 1 8 4. 5 \mathrm {l b}
$$

184.5 lb ÷ 212.8 lb/ft³ = .867 ft³, volume of fuel in expansion chamber at operating temperature.

Assuming that all fission product gases are held in the expansion chamber for 3 - 10 days, or until rate of generation just equals rate of decay of all unstable gaseous fission products, ignoring loss due to neutron absorption, at 125 MW power, heating rate in expansion chamber due to gas decay is about 150 kilowatts.

Decayed heat generation rate is about $7 - 1 / 2\%$ fission heat release.

ANALOG REPRESENTATION OF   
__________

Instantaneous gammas and capture gammas constitute about $6 \%$ more. As a part of the delayed heating is in the gaseous phase, and an exact calculation of gamma heating in liquid phase of expansion chamber is not within the scope of this project, an estimate of the liquid phase heating due to beta and gamma energy absorption in the expansion chamber is taken to be $5 \%$ of the total fission heat, divided by the total volume in the expansion chamber. Thus,

$$
\frac {1 2 5 \times 1 0 ^ {6} \text {w a t t s}}{4 5 \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}} \times . 8 6 7 \mathrm {f t} ^ {3} \times . 0 5 = 1. 2 0 \mathrm {k W}
$$

In the absence of flux data for the expansion chamber, a figure of 130 KW is assumed for fission heating in the chamber. The ball park estimate is founded on information obtained from Mr. Lackey of ORNL, and is derived from an estimate for the ART.

Therefore, estimated heat rate in expansion chamber is $150 + 120 + 130 \cong 400$ kW.

Assuming that fuel is bought into chamber at $1250^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ and experiences a $100^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ temperature rise before being expelled,

400 KW x 3415 BTU/hr kw = 1,366,000 BTU/hr.

1,366,000 BTU/hr $\div$ (.27 BTU/lb $^\circ \mathrm{F}$ x 100 $^{\circ} \mathrm{F}$ ) = 50,600 lb/hr, fuel flow required to remove heat.

If a temperature rise of $75^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ is experienced, 67,500 lb/hr will be required.

Assuming that inlet temperature is $1175^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , expansion tank exit temperature would be $1275^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ and average temperature $1225^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ with $100^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ rise.

Use of a helium purge for the system would result in reduction of

heating in expansion chamber by no more than $30\%$ .
